INTER-UNIVERSAL TEICHMUÌˆLLER THEORY I:
CONSTRUCTION OF HODGE THEATERS

Shinichi Mochizuki
May 2020
Abstract.
The present paper is the ï¬rst in a series of four papers, the
goal of which is to establish an arithmetic version of TeichmuÌˆller theory for number
ï¬elds equipped with an elliptic curve â€” which we refer to as â€œinter-universal
TeichmuÌˆller theoryâ€ â€” by applying the theory of semi-graphs of anabelioids,
Frobenioids, the eÌtale theta function, and log-shells developed in earlier papers by
the author. We begin by ï¬xing what we call â€œinitial Î˜-dataâ€, which consists of
an elliptic curve EF over a number ï¬eld F , and a prime number l â‰¥ 5, as well as
some other technical data satisfying certain technical properties. This data determines various hyperbolic orbicurves that are related via ï¬nite eÌtale coverings to the
once-punctured elliptic curve XF determined by EF . These ï¬nite eÌtale coverings
admit various symmetry properties arising from the additive and multiplicative
structures on the ring Fl = Z/lZ acting on the l-torsion points of the elliptic curve.
We then construct â€œÎ˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theatersâ€ associated to the given Î˜-data. These
Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theaters may be thought of as miniature models of conventional
scheme theory in which the two underlying combinatorial dimensions of a
number ï¬eld â€” which may be thought of as corresponding to the additive and
multiplicative structures of a ring or, alternatively, to the group of units and
value group of a local ï¬eld associated to the number ï¬eld â€” are, in some sense,
â€œdismantledâ€ or â€œdisentangledâ€ from one another. All Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theaters
are isomorphic to one another, but may also be related to one another by means of a
â€œÎ˜-linkâ€, which relates certain Frobenioid-theoretic portions of one Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge
theater to another in a fashion that is not compatible with the respective conventional ring/scheme theory structures. In particular, it is a highly nontrivial
problem to relate the ring structures on either side of the Î˜-link to one another. This
will be achieved, up to certain â€œrelatively mild indeterminaciesâ€, in future papers
in the series by applying the absolute anabelian geometry developed in earlier
papers by the author. The resulting description of an â€œalien ring structureâ€ [associated, say, to the domain of the Î˜-link] in terms of a given ring structure [associated,
say, to the codomain of the Î˜-link] will be applied in the ï¬nal paper of the series to
obtain results in diophantine geometry. Finally, we discuss certain technical results
concerning proï¬nite conjugates of decomposition and inertia groups in the tempered fundamental group of a p-adic hyperbolic curve that will be of use in the
development of the theory of the present series of papers, but are also of independent
interest.

Â§I1. Summary of Main Results
The present paper is the ï¬rst in a series of four papers, the goal of which is
to establish an arithmetic version of TeichmuÌˆller theory for number ï¬elds
equipped with an elliptic curve, by applying the theory of semi-graphs of anabelioids, Frobenioids, the eÌtale theta function, and log-shells developed in [SemiAnbd],
[FrdI], [FrdII], [EtTh], and [AbsTopIII] [cf., especially, [EtTh] and [AbsTopIII]].
Unlike many mathematical papers, which are devoted to verifying properties of
mathematical objects that are either well-known or easily constructed from wellknown mathematical objects, in the present series of papers, most of our eï¬€orts
will be devoted to constructing new mathematical objects. It is only in the ï¬nal
portion of the third paper in the series, i.e., [IUTchIII], that we turn to the task of
proving properties of interest concerning the mathematical objects constructed. In
the fourth paper of the series, i.e., [IUTchIV], we show that these properties may
be combined with certain elementary computations to obtain diophantine results
concerning elliptic curves over number ï¬elds.
We refer to Â§0 below for more on the notations and conventions applied in the

In this context, we remark that many of the constructions of [AbsTopIII] were
intended as prototypes for constructions of the present series of papers. For instance, the global theory of [AbsTopIII], Â§5, was intended as a sort of simpliï¬ed
prototype for the Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theaters of the present paper, i.e., except with
the various label bookkeeping devices deleted. The various panalocal objects of [AbsTopIII], Â§5, were intended as prototypes for the various types of prime-strips that

8

appear in the present series of papers. Perhaps most importantly, the theory of the
log-Frobenius functor and log-shells developed in [AbsTopIII], Â§3, Â§4, Â§5, was intended as a prototype for the theory of the log-link that is developed in [IUTchIII].
In particular, although most of the main ideas and techniques of [AbsTopIII],
Â§3, Â§4, Â§5, will play an important role in the present series of papers, many of the
constructions performed in [AbsTopIII], Â§3, Â§4, Â§5, will not be applied in a direct,
literal sense in the present series of papers.
The FÂ±
l -symmetry has the advantange that, being geometric in nature, it
allows one to permute various copies of â€œGv â€ [where v âˆˆ Vnon ] associated to distinct labels âˆˆ Fl without inducing conjugacy indeterminacies. This phenomenon,
which we shall refer to as conjugate synchronization, will play a key role in
the Kummer theory surrounding the Hodge-Arakelov-theoretic evaluation of the
theta function at l-torsion points that is developed in [IUTchII]â€” cf. the discussion of Remark 6.12.6; [IUTchII], Remark 3.5.2, (ii), (iii); [IUTchII], Remark
4.5.3, (i). By contrast, the F
l -symmetry is more suited to situations in which one
must descend from K to Fmod . In the present series of papers, the most important
such situation involves the Kummer theory surrounding the reconstruction of
the number ï¬eld Fmod from the eÌtale fundamental group of C K â€” cf. the discussion of Remark 6.12.6; [IUTchII], Remark 4.7.6. This reconstruction will be
discussed in Example 5.1 of the present paper. Here, we note that such situations
necessarily induce global Galois permutations of the various copies of â€œGv â€ [where
v âˆˆ Vnon ] associated to distinct labels âˆˆ F

l -symmetry is ill-suited to situations,
such as those that appear in the theory of Hodge-Arakelov-theoretic evaluation that
is developed in [IUTchII], that require one to establish conjugate synchronization.

Ultimately, when, in [IUTchIV], we consider diophantine applications of the
theory developed in the present series of papers, we will take the prime number
l to be â€œlargeâ€, i.e., roughly of the order of the square root of the height of the
elliptic curve EF [cf. [IUTchIV], Corollary 2.2, (ii), (C1)]. When l is regarded as
large, the arithmetic of the ï¬nite ï¬eld Fl â€œtends to approximateâ€ the arithmetic of
the ring of rational integers Z. That is to say, the decomposition that occurs in
a Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theater into the â€œadditiveâ€ [i.e., FÂ±

in the theory of Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theaters may be compared to groups of additive and multiplicative symmetries of the upper half-plane [cf. Fig. I1.4
below]. Here, the â€œcuspidalâ€ geometry expressed by the additive symmetries of
the upper half-plane admits a natural â€œassociated coordinateâ€, namely, the classical q-parameter, which is reminiscent of the way in which the FÂ±

is well-adapted to the Kummer theory surrounding the Hodge-Arakelov-theoretic
evaluation of the theta function at l-torsion points [cf. the above discussion].
By contrast, the â€œtoralâ€, or â€œnodalâ€ [cf. the classical theory of the structure of
Hecke correspondences modulo p], geometry expressed by the multiplicative symmetries of the upper half-plane admits a natural â€œassociated coordinateâ€, namely,
the classical biholomorphic isomorphism of the upper half-plane with the unit disc,
which is reminiscent of the way in which the F
l -symmetry is well-adapted to the
Kummer theory surrounding the number ï¬eld Fmod [cf. the above discussion].
For more details, we refer to the discussion of Remark 6.12.3, (iii).
From the point of view of the scheme-theoretic Hodge-Arakelov theory developed in [HASurI], [HASurII], the theory of the combinatorial structure of a Î˜Â±ell NFHodge theater â€” and, indeed, the theory of the present series of papers! â€” may
be regarded as a sort of
solution to the problem of constructing â€œglobal multiplicative subspacesâ€ and â€œglobal canonical generatorsâ€ [cf. the quotient â€œQâ€ and
the cusp â€œâ€ that appear in the above discussion!]
â€” the nonexistence of which in a â€œnaive, scheme-theoretic senseâ€ constitutes the
main obstruction to applying the theory of [HASurI], [HASurII] to diophantine
geometry [cf. the discussion of Remark 4.3.1]. Indeed, prime-strips may be
thought of as â€œlocal analytic sectionsâ€ of the natural morphism Spec(K) â†’
Spec(Fmod ). Thus, it is precisely by working with such â€œlocal analytic sectionsâ€ â€”
i.e., more concretely, by working with the collection of valuations V, as opposed to
the set of all valuations of K â€” that one can, in some sense, â€œsimulateâ€ the notions
of a â€œglobal multiplicative subspaceâ€ or a â€œglobal canonical generatorâ€. On the other
hand, such â€œsimulated global objectsâ€ may only be achieved at the cost of
â€œdismantlingâ€, or performing â€œsurgeryâ€ on, the global prime structure of the number ï¬elds involved [cf. the discussion of Remark 4.3.1]
â€” a quite drastic operation, which has the eï¬€ect of precipitating numerous technical
diï¬ƒculties, whose resolution, via the theory of semi-graphs of anabelioids, Frobenioids, the eÌtale theta function, and log-shells developed in [SemiAnbd], [FrdI],
[FrdII], [EtTh], and [AbsTopIII], constitutes the bulk of the theory of the present
series of papers! From the point of view of â€œperforming surgery on the global prime

In addition to the main result discussed above, we also prove a certain technical
result concerning tempered fundamental groups â€” cf. Theorem B below â€”
that will be of use in our development of the theory of Hodge-Arakelov-theoretic
evaluation in [IUTchII]. This result is essentially a routine application of the theory of maximal compact subgroups of tempered fundamental groups developed in
[SemiAnbd] [cf., especially, [SemiAnbd], Theorems 3.7, 5.4, as well as Remark 2.5.3,
(ii), of the present paper]. Here, we recall that this theory of [SemiAnbd] may be
thought of as a sort of â€œCombinatorial Section Conjectureâ€ [cf. Remark 2.5.1
of the present paper; [IUTchII], Remark 1.12.4] â€” a point of view that is of particular interest in light of the historical remarks made in Â§I5 below. Moreover, Theorem
B is of interest independently of the theory of the present series of papers in that
it yields, for instance, a new proof of the normal terminality of the tempered fundamental group in its proï¬nite completion, a result originally obtained in [AndreÌ],
Lemma 3.2.1, by means of other techniques [cf. Remark 2.4.1]. This new proof
is of interest in that, unlike the techniques of [AndreÌ], which are only available in
the proï¬nite case, this new proof [cf. Proposition 2.4, (iii)] holds in the case of
pro-Î£-completions, for more general Î£ [i.e., not just the case of Î£ = Primes].

Finally, we remark that one important reason for the need to apply Theorem B
in the context of the theory of Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theaters summarized in Theorem A
is the following. The FÂ±

at v âˆˆ Vbad [i.e., where the crucial Hodge-Arakelov-theoretic evaluation is to be

Â§I3. Basepoints and Inter-universality
As discussed in Â§I2, the present series of papers is concerned with considering
â€œdeformations of the arithmetic holomorphic structureâ€ of a number ï¬eld â€” i.e., so
to speak, with performing â€œsurgery on the number ï¬eldâ€. At a more concrete
level, this means that one must consider situations in which two distinct â€œtheatersâ€
for conventional ring/scheme theory â€” i.e., two distinct Î˜Â±ell NF-Hodge theaters
â€” are related to one another by means of a â€œcorrespondenceâ€, or â€œï¬lterâ€, that fails
to be compatible with the respective ring structures. In the discussion so far of
the portion of the theory developed in the present paper, the main example of such
a â€œï¬lterâ€ is given by the Î˜-link. As mentioned earlier, more elaborate versions
of the Î˜-link will be discussed in [IUTchII], [IUTchIII]. The other main example
of such a non-ring/scheme-theoretic â€œï¬lterâ€ in the present series of papers is the
log-link, which we shall discuss in [IUTchIII] [cf. also the theory of [AbsTopIII]].
One important aspect of such non-ring/scheme-theoretic ï¬lters is the property
that they are incompatible with various constructions that depend on the ring
structure of the theaters that constitute the domain and codomain of such a ï¬lter.
From the point of view of the present series of papers, perhaps the most important example of such a construction is given by the various eÌtale fundamental
groups â€” e.g., Galois groups â€” that appear in these theaters. Indeed, these
groups are deï¬ned, essentially, as automorphism groups of some separably closed
ï¬eld, i.e., the ï¬eld that arises in the deï¬nition of the ï¬ber functor associated to the
basepoint determined by a geometric point that is used to deï¬ne the eÌtale fundamental group â€” cf. the discussion of [IUTchII], Remark 3.6.3, (i); [IUTchIII],
Remark 1.2.4, (i); [AbsTopIII], Remark 3.7.7, (i). In particular, unlike the case
with ring homomorphisms or morphisms of schemes with respect to which the eÌtale
fundamental group satisï¬es well-known functoriality properties, in the case of nonring/scheme-theoretic ï¬lters, the only â€œtype of mathematical objectâ€ that makes
sense simultaneously in both the domain and codomain theaters of the ï¬lter is the
notion of a topological group. In particular, the only data that can be considered in
relating eÌtale fundamental groups on either side of a ï¬lter is the eÌtale-like structure constituted by the underlying abstract topological group associated to
such an eÌtale fundamental group, i.e., devoid of any auxiliary data arising from the
construction of the group â€œas an eÌtale fundamental group associated to a basepoint determined by a geometric point of a schemeâ€. It is this fundamental aspect
of the theory of the present series of papers â€” i.e.,
of relating the distinct set-theoretic universes associated to the distinct
ï¬ber functors/basepoints on either side of such a non-ring/scheme-theoretic
ï¬lter
â€” that we refer to as inter-universal. This inter-universal aspect of the theory
manifestly leads to the issue of considering
the extent to which one can understand various ring/scheme structures
by considering only the underlying abstract topological group of some
eÌtale fundamental group arising from such a ring/scheme structure
â€” i.e., in other words, of considering the absolute anabelian geometry [cf. the
Introductions to [AbsTopI], [AbsTopII], [AbsTopIII]] of the rings/schemes under
consideration.

22

SHINICHI MOCHIZUKI

At this point, the careful reader will note that the above discussion of the
inter-universal aspects of the theory of the present series of papers depends, in
an essential way, on the issue of distinguishing diï¬€erent â€œtypes of mathematical
objectsâ€ and hence, in particular, on the notion of a â€œtype of mathematical objectâ€.
This notion may be formalized via the language of â€œspeciesâ€, which we develop
in the ï¬nal portion of [IUTchIV].
Another important â€œinter-universalâ€ phenomenon in the present series of papers â€” i.e., phenomenon which, like the absolute anabelian aspects discussed above,
arises from a â€œdeep sensitivity to particular choices of basepointsâ€ â€” is the phenomenon of conjugate synchronization, i.e., of synchronization between conjugacy indeterminacies of distinct copies of various local Galois groups, which, as was
mentioned in Â§I1, will play an important role in the theory of [IUTchII], [IUTchIII].
The various rigidity properties of the eÌtale theta function established in [EtTh]
constitute yet another inter-universal phenomenon that will play an important role
in theory of [IUTchII], [IUTchIII].

Â§I4. Relation to Complex and p-adic TeichmuÌˆller Theory
In order to understand the sense in which the theory of the present series
of papers may be thought of as a sort of â€œTeichmuÌˆller theoryâ€ of number ï¬elds

concerning the classical complex TeichmuÌˆller theory of Riemann surfaces of
ï¬nite type [cf., e.g., [Lehto], Chapter V, Â§8]. Although such a Riemann surface is
one-dimensional from a complex, holomorphic point of view, this single complex
dimension may be thought of consisting of two underlying real analytic dimensions.
Relative to a suitable canonical holomorphic coordinate z = x + iy on the Riemann
surface, the TeichmuÌˆller deformation may be written in the form

the theory of such TeichmuÌˆller deformations may be summarized as consisting of
the explicit description of a varying holomorphic structure within a

On the other hand, as discussed in [AbsTopIII], Â§I3, one may think of the ring

tautological once-punctured elliptic curve over the moduli stack of [oncepunctured] elliptic curves?

Â§I5. Other Galois-theoretic Approaches to Diophantine Geometry
The notion of anabelian geometry dates back to a famous â€œletter to Faltingsâ€ [cf. [Groth]], written by Grothendieck in response to Faltingsâ€™ work on the
Mordell Conjecture [cf. [Falt]]. Anabelian geometry was apparently originally conceived by Grothendieck as a new approach to obtaining results in diophantine
geometry such as the Mordell Conjecture. At the time of writing, the author is
not aware of any expositions by Grothendieck that expose this approach in detail.
Nevertheless, it appears that the thrust of this approach revolves around applying
the Section Conjecture for hyperbolic curves over number ï¬elds to obtain a contradiction by applying this Section Conjecture to the â€œlimit sectionâ€ of the Galois
sections associated to any inï¬nite sequence of rational points of a proper hyperbolic
curve over a number ï¬eld [cf. [MNT], Â§4.1(B), for more details]. On the other hand,
to the knowledge of the author, at least at the time of writing, it does not appear
that any rigorous argument has been obtained either by Grothendieck or by other
mathematicians for deriving a new proof of the Mordell Conjecture from the [as
yet unproven] Section Conjecture for hyperbolic curves over number ï¬elds. Nevertheless, one result that has been obtained is a new proof by M. Kim [cf. [Kim]]
of Siegelâ€™s theorem concerning Q-rational points of the projective line minus three
points â€” a proof which proceeds by obtaining certain bounds on the cardinality
of the set of Galois sections, without applying the Section Conjecture or any other
results from anabelian geometry.
In light of the historical background just discussed, the theory exposed in
the present series of papers â€” which yields, in particular, a method for applying
results in absolute anabelian geometry to obtain diophantine results such
as those given in [IUTchIV] â€” occupies a somewhat curious position, relative to
the historical development of the mathematical ideas involved. That is to say, at a
purely formal level, the implication
anabelian geometry

=â‡’

diophantine results

at ï¬rst glance looks something like a â€œconï¬rmationâ€ of Grothendieckâ€™s original
intuition. On the other hand, closer inspection reveals that the approach of the
theory of the present series of papers â€” that is to say, the precise content of the
relationship between anabelian geometry and diophantine geometry established in

INTER-UNIVERSAL TEICHMUÌˆLLER THEORY I

29

the present series of papers â€” diï¬€ers quite fundamentally from the sort of approach
that was apparently envisioned by Grothendieck.
Perhaps the most characteristic aspect of this diï¬€erence lies in the central role
played by anabelian geometry over p-adic ï¬elds in the present series of papers.
That is to say, unlike the case with number ï¬elds, one central feature of anabelian
geometry over p-adic ï¬elds is the fundamental gap between relative and absolute
results [cf., e.g., [AbsTopI], Introduction]. This fundamental gap is closely related
to the notion of an â€œarithmetic TeichmuÌˆller theory for number ï¬eldsâ€ [cf.
the discussion of Â§I4 of the present paper; [AbsTopIII], Â§I3, Â§I5] â€” i.e., a theory of
deformations not for the â€œarithmetic holomorphic structureâ€ of a hyperbolic curve
over a number ï¬eld, but rather for the â€œarithmetic holomorphic structureâ€ of the
number ï¬eld itself! To the knowledge of the author, there does not exist any mention
of such ideas [i.e., relative vs. absolute p-adic anabelian geometry; the notion of an
arithmetic TeichmuÌˆller theory for number ï¬elds] in the works of Grothendieck.
As discussed in Â§I4, one fundamental theme of the theory of the present series
of papers is the issue of the
explicit description of the relationship between the additive structure and
the multiplicative structure of a ring/number ï¬eld/local ï¬eld.
Relative to the above discussion of the relationship between anabelian geometry
and diophantine geometry, it is of interest to note that this issue of understanding/describing the relationship between addition and multiplication is, on the one
hand, a central theme in the proofs of various results in anabelian geometry [cf.,
e.g., [Tama1], [pGC], [AbsTopIII]] and, on the other hand, a central aspect of the
diophantine results obtained in [IUTchIV].
From a historical point of view, it is also of interest to note that results from absolute anabelian geometry are applied in the present series of papers in the context
of the canonical splittings of the Frobenius-picture that arise by considering the
eÌtale-picture [cf. the discussion in Â§I1 preceding Theorem A]. This state of aï¬€airs
is reminiscent â€” relative to the point of view that the Grothendieck Conjecture
constitutes a sort of â€œanabelian versionâ€ of the Tate Conjecture for abelian varieties
[cf. the discussion of [MNT], Â§1.2] â€” of the role played by the Tate Conjecture for
abelian varieties in obtaining the diophantine results of [Falt], namely, by means
of the various semi-simplicity properties of the Tate module that arise as formal
consequences of the Tate Conjecture. That is to say, such semi-simplicity properties may also be thought of as â€œcanonical splittingsâ€ that arise from Galois-theoretic
considerations [cf. the discussion of â€œcanonical splittingsâ€ in the ï¬nal portion of
[CombCusp], Introduction].
Certain aspects of the relationship between the inter-universal TeichmuÌˆller
theory of the present series of papers and other Galois-theoretic approaches to diophantine geometry are best understood in the context of the analogy, discussed in
Â§I4, between inter-universal TeichmuÌˆller theory and p-adic TeichmuÌˆller theory.

In the context of these two distinct possible directions for the further development of the p-adic TeichmuÌˆller theory of [pOrd], [pTeich], it is of interest to recall
the following elementary fact:
If G is a free pro-p group of rank â‰¥ 2, then a [continuous] representation
ÏG : G â†’ GLn (Qp )
can never be injective!
Indeed, assume that ÏG is injective and write . . . âŠ† Hj âŠ† . . . âŠ† Im(ÏG ) âŠ† GLn (Qp )
for an exhaustive sequence of open normal subgroups of the image of ÏG . Then since

INTER-UNIVERSAL TEICHMUÌˆLLER THEORY I

31

the Hj are closed subgroups of GLn (Qp ), hence p-adic Lie groups, it follows that the
Qp -dimension dim(Hjab âŠ— Qp ) of the tensor product with Qp of the abelianization
of Hj may be computed at the level of Lie algebras, hence is bounded by the Qp dimension of the p-adic Lie group GLn (Qp ), i.e., we have dim(Hjab âŠ— Qp ) â‰¤ n2 , in
contradiction to the well-known fact since G âˆ¼

holds that dim(Hjab âŠ— Qp ) â†’ âˆ as j â†’ âˆ. Note, moreover, that
this sort of argument, i.e., concerning the asymptotic behavior of the
abelianizations â€” or, more generally, of the Lie algebras associated to
the pro-algebraic groups determined by associated Tannakian categories
of representations â€” of open subgroups, is characteristic of the sort of
proofs that typically occur in anabelian geometry [cf., e.g., the proofs
of [Tama1], [pGC], [CombGC], as well as [Cusp], Â§3!].
That is to say, the above argument to the eï¬€ect that ÏG can never be injective is a
typical instance of the more general phenomenon that
so long as one restricts oneself to representation theory into GLn (Qp )
[or even more general groups that arise as groups of Qp -valued points
of pro-algebraic groups], one can never access the sort of asymptotic
phenomena that form the â€œtechnical coreâ€ [cf., e.g., the proofs of
[Tama1], [pGC], [CombGC], as well as [Cusp], Â§3!] of various important
results in anabelian geometry.
Put another way, the two â€œdirectionsâ€ discussed above â€” i.e., representationtheoretic and anabelian â€” appear to be essentially mutually alien to one
another.
In this context, it is of interest to observe that the diophantine results derived in [IUTchIV] from the inter-universal TeichmuÌˆller theory developed in the
present series of papers concern essentially asymptotic behavior, i.e., they do
not concern properties of â€œa speciï¬c rational point over a speciï¬c number ï¬eldâ€,
but rather properties of the asymptotic behavior of â€œvarying rational points over
varying number ï¬eldsâ€. One important aspect of this asymptotic nature of the diophantine results derived in [IUTchIV] is that there are no distinguished number
ï¬elds that occur in the theory, i.e., the theory â€” being essentially asymptotic in
nature! â€” is â€œinvariantâ€ with respect to the operation of passing to ï¬nite extensions of the number ï¬eld involved [which, from the point of view of the absolute
Galois group GQ of Q, corresponds precisely to the operation of passing to smaller
and smaller open subgroups, as in the above discussion!]. This contrasts sharply
with the â€œrepresentation-theoretic approach to diophantine geometryâ€ constituted
by such works as [Wiles], where speciï¬c rational points over the speciï¬c number ï¬eld
Q â€” or, for instance, in generalizations of [Wiles] involving Shimura varieties, over
speciï¬c number ï¬elds characteristically associated to the Shimura varieties involved
â€” play a central role.

Remark 2.5.1. The content of Corollary 2.5 may be regarded as a sort of [very
weak!] version of the â€œSection Conjectureâ€ of anabelian geometry â€” i.e., as the
assertion that certain sections of the tempered fundamental group [namely, those
that arise from geometric sections of the proï¬nite fundamental group] are geometric
as sections of the tempered fundamental group. This point of view is reminiscent
of the point of view of [SemiAnbd], Remark 6.9.1. Perhaps one way of summarizing
this circle of ideas is to state that one may think of
(i) the classiï¬cation of maximal compact subgroups of tempered fundamental
groups given in [SemiAnbd], Theorem 3.7, (iv); [SemiAnbd], Theorem 5.4,
(ii) [cf. also Remark 2.5.3, (ii), (E5), (E7), of the present paper], or, for
that matter,
(ii) the more elementary fact that â€œany ï¬nite group acting on a tree [without
inversion] ï¬xes at least one vertexâ€ [cf. [SemiAnbd], Lemma 1.8, (ii)] from
which these results of [SemiAnbd] are derived
as a sort of combinatorial version of the Section Conjecture.
Remark 2.5.2. Ultimately, when we apply Corollary 2.5 in [IUTchII], it will
only be necessary to apply the portion of Corollary 2.5 that concerns inertia groups
of cusps, i.e., the portion whose proof only requires the use of Proposition 2.4,
(i), which is essentially an immediate consequence of Proposition 2.1. That is to
say, the theory developed in [IUTchII] [and indeed throughout the present series of
papers] will never require the application of Proposition 2.4, (ii), i.e., whose proof
depends on a slightly more complicated version of the proof of Proposition 2.1.
Remark 2.5.3. In light of the importance of the theory of [SemiAnbd] in the
present Â§2, we pause to discuss certain minor oversights on the part of the author
in the exposition of [SemiAnbd].
(i) Certain pathologies occur in the theory of tempered fundamental groups
if one does not impose suitable countability hypotheses. In order to discuss these
countability hypotheses, it will be convenient to introduce some terminology as
follows:
(T1) We shall say that a tempered group is Galois-countable if its topology admits a countable basis. We shall say that a connected temperoid
is Galois-countable if it arises from a Galois-countable tempered group.
We shall say that a temperoid is Galois-countable if it arises from a collection of Galois-countable connected temperoids. We shall say that a
connected quasi-temperoid is Galois-countable if it arises from a Galoiscountable connected temperoid. We shall say that a quasi-temperoid is
Galois-countable if it arises from a collection of Galois-countable connected
quasi-temperoids.
(T2) We shall say that a semi-graph of anabelioids G is Galois-countable if it
is countable, and, moreover, admits a countable collection of ï¬nite eÌtale
coverings {Gi â†’ G}iâˆˆI such that for any ï¬nite eÌtale covering H â†’ G,
there exists an i âˆˆ I such that the base-changed covering H Ã—G Gi â†’ Gi
splits over the constituent anabelioid associated to each component of [the
underlying semi-graph of] Gi .

INTER-UNIVERSAL TEICHMUÌˆLLER THEORY I

53

(T3) We shall say that a semi-graph of anabelioids G is strictly coherent if it
is coherent [cf. [SemiAnbd], Deï¬nition 2.3, (iii)], and, moreover, each of
the proï¬nite groups associated to components c of [the underlying semigraph of] G [cf. the ï¬nal sentence of [SemiAnbd], Deï¬nition 2.3, (iii)] is
topologically generated by N generators, for some positive integer N that
is independent of c. In particular, it follows that if G is ï¬nite and coherent,
then it is strictly coherent.
(T4) One veriï¬es immediately that every strictly coherent, countable semigraph of anabelioids is Galois-countable.
(T5) One veriï¬es immediately that if, in [SemiAnbd], Remark 3.2.1, one assumes in addition that the temperoid X is Galois-countable, then it follows
that its associated tempered fundamental group Ï€1temp (X ) is well-deï¬ned
and Galois-countable.
(T6) One veriï¬es immediately that if, in the discussion of the paragraph
preceding [SemiAnbd], Proposition 3.6, one assumes in addition that the
semi-graph of anabelioids G is Galois-countable, then it follows that its
associated tempered fundamental group Ï€1temp (G) and temperoid B temp (G)
are well-deï¬ned and Galois-countable.
Here, we note that, in (T5) and (T6), the Galois-countability assumption is necessary in order to ensure that the index sets of â€œuniversal covering pro-objectsâ€
implicit in the deï¬nition of the tempered fundamental group may to be taken to
be countable. This countability of the index sets involved implies that the various
objects that constitute such a universal covering pro-object admit a compatible system of basepoints, i.e., that the obstruction to the existence of such a compatible
system â€” which may be thought of as an element of a sort of â€œnonabelian R1 lim
â†âˆ’â€
â€” vanishes. In order to deï¬ne the tempered fundamental group in an intrinsically meaningful fashion, it is necessary to know the existence of such a compatible
system of basepoints.

Next, we consider assertion (iv). By assertion (i), it follows that any abelian

Section 3: Chains of Î˜-Hodge Theaters
In the present Â§3, we construct chains of â€œÎ˜-Hodge theatersâ€. Each â€œÎ˜-Hodge
theaterâ€ is to be thought of as a sort of miniature model of the conventional
scheme-theoretic arithmetic geometry that surrounds the theta function.
This miniature model is formulated via the theory of Frobenioids [cf. [FrdI]; [FrdII];
[EtTh], Â§3, Â§4, Â§5]. On the other hand, the link [cf. Corollary 3.7, (i)] between
adjacent members of such chains is purely Frobenioid-theoretic, i.e., it lies outside
the framework of ring theory/scheme theory. It is these chains of Î˜-Hodge theaters
that form the starting point of the theory of the present series of papers.

Remark 3.1.5. Note that since the 3-torsion points of EF are rational over F ,

the ï¬eld Fmod and various extensions of Fmod contained in F , we shall also have
occasion to work with the algebraic stack

algebraic stack Smod,L over Smod . In particular, by considering arithmetic line bundles over such Smod,L , one may associate to any ï¬nite quotient Gal(F /Fmod )  Q
a Frobenioid via [the easily veriï¬ed â€œstack-theoretic versionâ€ of] the construction

Remark 3.1.6. In light of the important role played by the various orbicurves
constructed in [EtTh], Â§2, in the present series of papers, we take the opportunity
to correct an unfortunate â€” albeit in fact irrelevant! â€” error in [EtTh]. In the
discussion preceding [EtTh], Deï¬nition 2.1, one must in fact assume that the integer
l is odd in order for the quotient Î”X to be well-deï¬ned. Since, ultimately, in [EtTh]
[cf. the discussion following [EtTh], Remark 5.7.1], as well as in the present series
of papers, this is the only case that is of interest, this oversight does not aï¬€ect
either the present series of papers or the bulk of the remainder of [EtTh]. Indeed,
the only places in [EtTh] where the case of even l is used are [EtTh], Remark 2.2.1,
and the application of [EtTh], Remark 2.2.1, in the proof of [EtTh], Proposition
2.12, for the orbicurves â€œCÌ‡â€. Thus, [EtTh], Remark 2.2.1, must be deleted; in
[EtTh], Proposition 2.12, one must in fact exclude the case where the orbicurve
under consideration is â€œCÌ‡â€. On the other hand, this theory involving [EtTh],
Proposition 2.12 [cf., especially, [EtTh], Corollaries 2.18, 2.19] is only applied after
the discussion following [EtTh], Remark 5.7.1, i.e., which only treats the curves
â€œXâ€. That is to say, ultimately, in [EtTh], as well as in the present series of papers,
one is only interested in the curves â€œXâ€, whose treatment only requires the case of

Remark 3.1.7.

Let L be a ï¬eld which is equal either to Fmod or to (Fmod )v for some v âˆˆ Vmod .

Â· whenever f âˆˆ L, it holds that, over L, f has precisely one pole [of

that both f and f âˆ’1 are Îº-coric. By contrast, the third displayed condition may be
understood as the condition that restriction to the strictly critical points determines

follows from the third displayed condition that if c âˆˆ L and f âˆˆ LC are such that
both f and c Â· f are Îº-coric, then c is a root of unity.

Remark 3.2.1.
(i) In [FrdI], [FrdII], and [EtTh] [cf. [EtTh], Remark 5.1.1], the phrase â€œreconstructed category-theoreticallyâ€ is interpreted as meaning â€œpreserved by equivalences
of categoriesâ€. From the point of view of the theory of [AbsTopIII] â€” i.e., the discussion of â€œmono-anabelianâ€ versus â€œbi-anabelianâ€ geometry [cf. [AbsTopIII], Â§I2,

74

SHINICHI MOCHIZUKI

(Q2)] â€” this sort of deï¬nition is â€œbi-anabelianâ€ in nature. In fact, it is not diï¬ƒcult
to verify that the techniques of [FrdI], [FrdII], and [EtTh] all result in explicit reconstruction algorithms, whose input data consists solely of the category structure
of the given category, of a â€œmono-anabelianâ€ nature that do not require the use of
some ï¬xed reference model that arises from scheme theory [cf. the discussion of
[AbsTopIII], Â§I4]. For more on the foundational aspects of such â€œmono-anabelian
reconstruction algorithmsâ€, we refer to the discussion of [IUTchIV], Example 3.5.
(ii) One reason that we do not develop in detail here a â€œmono-anabelian approach to the geometry of categoriesâ€ along the lines of [AbsTopIII] is that, unlike
the case with the mono-anabelian theory of [AbsTopIII], which plays a quite essential role in the theory of the present series of papers, much of the category-theoretic
reconstruction theory of [FrdI], [FrdII], and [EtTh] is not of essential importance
in the development of the theory of the present series of papers. That is to say, for
instance, instead of quoting results to the eï¬€ect that the base categories or divisor
monoids of various Frobenioids may be reconstructed category-theoretically, one
could instead simply work with the data consisting of â€œthe category constituted by
the Frobenioid equipped with its pre-Frobenioid structureâ€ [cf. [FrdI], Deï¬nition
1.1, (iv)]. Nevertheless, we chose to apply the theory of [FrdI], [FrdII], and [EtTh]
partly because it simpliï¬es the exposition [i.e., reduces the number of auxiliary
structures that one must carry around], but more importantly because it renders
explicit precisely which structures arising from scheme-theory are â€œcategorically
intrinsicâ€ and which merely amount to â€œarbitrary, non-intrinsic choicesâ€ which,
when formulated intrinsically, correspond to various â€œindeterminaciesâ€. This explicitness is of particular importance with respect to phenomena related to the unitlinear Frobenius functor [cf. [FrdI], Proposition 2.5] and the Frobenioid-theoretic
indeterminacies studied in [EtTh], Â§5.

(iv) The problem pointed out in (iii) may be remedied â€” at least from the

(v) In the discussion of (iv), we note that the approach of (A) results in a
slightly diï¬€erent deï¬nition of the notion of a â€œtempered Frobenioidâ€ from the original

deï¬nition given in [EtTh]. Put another way, the approach of (B) has the advantage

â€œtempered Frobenioidâ€; that is to say, the approach of (B) only results in a slight

of arbitrary rational functions of the tempered Frobenioid without imposing any
further hypotheses. Thus, for the sake of simplicity, in the present series of papers,
we shall interpret the notion of a â€œtempered Frobenioidâ€ via the approach of (A).

Remark 3.4.1. A similar remark to Remark 3.2.1 [i.e., concerning the phrase
â€œreconstructed category-theoreticallyâ€] applies to the phrase â€œalgorithmically reconstructedâ€ that was applied in the discussion of Example 3.4.
Remark 3.4.2. One way to think of the Kummer structure
Îºv : O (Cv ) â†’ ADv
discussed in Example 3.4, (i), is as follows. In the terminology of [AbsTopIII], Definition 2.1, (i), (iv), the structure of CAF on ADv determines, via pull-back by Îºv ,
an Aut-holomorphic structure on the groupiï¬cation O (Cv )gp of O (Cv ), together
with a [tautological!] co-holomorphicization O (Cv )gp â†’ ADv . Conversely, if one
starts with this Aut-holomorphic structure on [the groupiï¬cation of] the topological
monoid O (Cv ), together with the co-holomorphicization O (Cv )gp â†’ ADv , then
one veriï¬es immediately that one may recover the inclusion of topological monoids
Îºv . [Indeed, this follows immediately from [AbsTopIII], Corollary 2.3, together
with the elementary fact that every holomorphic automorphism of the complex Lie
group CÃ— that preserves the submonoid of elements of norm â‰¤ 1 is equal to the
identity.] That is to say, in summary,
the Kummer structure Îºv is completely equivalent to the collection
of data consisting of the Aut-holomorphic structure [induced by Îºv ] on
the groupiï¬cation O (Cv )gp of O (Cv ), together with the co-holomorphicization [induced by Îºv ] O (Cv )gp â†’ ADv .
The signiï¬cance of thinking of Kummer structures in this way lies in the observation
that [unlike inclusions of topological monoids!]
the co-holomorphicization induced by Îºv is compatible with the logarithm operation discussed in [AbsTopIII], Corollary 4.5.
Indeed, this observation may be thought of as a rough summary of a substantial
portion of the content of [AbsTopIII], Corollary 4.5. Put another way, thinking of
Kummer structures in terms of co-holomorphicizations allows one to separate out
the portion of the structures involved that is not compatible with this logarithm
operation â€” i.e., the monoid structures! â€” from the portion of the structures
involved that is compatible with this logarithm operation â€” i.e., the tautological

Remark 3.9.3.
(i) As discussed in [AbsTopIII], Â§I3; [AbsTopIII], Remark 5.10.2, (ii), the
â€œmono-analytic coreâ€ {Dv }vâˆˆV may be thought of as a sort of ï¬xed underlying real-analytic surface associated to a number ï¬eld on which various holomorphic structures are imposed. Then the Frobenius-picture in its entirety may
be thought of as a sort of global arithmetic analogue of the notion of a TeichmuÌˆller geodesic in classical complex TeichmuÌˆller theory or, alternatively, as a
global arithmetic analogue of the canonical liftings of p-adic TeichmuÌˆller theory
[cf. the discussion of [AbsTopIII], Â§I5].
(ii) Recall that in classical complex TeichmuÌˆller theory, one of the two real dimensions of the surface is dilated as one moves along a TeichmuÌˆller geodesic, while
the other of the two real dimensions is held ï¬xed. In the case of the Frobeniuspicture of Corollary 3.8, the local units â€œOÃ— â€ correspond to the dimension that
is held ï¬xed, while the local value groups are subject to â€œÎ˜-dilationsâ€ as one
moves along the diagram constituted by the Frobenius-picture. Note that in order
to construct such a mathematical structure in which the local units and local value
groups are treated independently, it is of crucial importance to avail oneself of
the various characteristic splittings that appear in the split Frobenioids of Examples 3.2, 3.3, 3.4. Here, we note in passing that, in the case of Example 3.2,
this splitting corresponds to the â€œconstant multiple rigidityâ€ of the eÌtale theta
function, which forms a central theme of the theory of [EtTh].
(iii) In classical complex TeichmuÌˆller theory, the two real dimensions of the
surface that are treated independently of one another correspond to the real and
imaginary parts of the coordinate obtained by locally integrating the square root
of a given square diï¬€erential. In particular, it is of crucial importance in classical
complex TeichmuÌˆller theory that these real and imaginary parts not be â€œsubject to
confusion with one anotherâ€. In the case of the square root of a square diï¬€erential,
the only indeterminacy that arises is indeterminacy with respect to multiplication
by âˆ’1, an operation that satisï¬es the crucial property of preserving the real and
imaginary parts of a complex number. By contrast, it is interesting to note that
if, for n â‰¥ 3, one attempts to construct TeichmuÌˆller deformations in the
fashion of classical complex TeichmuÌˆller theory by means of coordinates
obtained by locally integrating the n-th root of a given section of the nth tensor power of the sheaf of diï¬€erentials, then one must contend with
an indeterminacy with respect to multiplication by an n-th root of unity,
an operation that results in an essential confusion between the real and
imaginary parts of a complex number.

(ii) The way in which these â€œarithmetic local-analytic sectionsâ€ constituted

primes in extensions of number ï¬elds. The decomposition of primes in extensions

(iii) In some sense, understanding more precisely the content of the failure of
these â€œarithmetic local-analytic sectionsâ€ constituted by the D-prime-strips to be

Remark 4.3.2. The incompatibility of the â€œarithmetic local-analytic sectionsâ€ of
Remark 4.3.1, (i), with global prime distributions and global absolute Galois groups
[cf. the discussion of Remark 4.3.1, (ii)] is precisely the technical obstacle that

INTER-UNIVERSAL TEICHMUÌˆLLER THEORY I

103

will necessitate the application â€” in [IUTchIII] â€” of the absolute p-adic monoanabelian geometry developed in [AbsTopIII], in the form of â€œpanalocalization along
the various prime-stripsâ€ [cf. [IUTchIII] for more details]. Indeed,
the mono-anabelian theory developed in [AbsTopIII] represents the culmination of earlier research of the author during the years 2000 to 2007
concerning absolute p-adic anabelian geometry â€” research that was
motivated precisely by the goal of developing a geometry that would allow
one to work with the â€œarithmetic local-analytic sectionsâ€ constituted by
the prime-strips, so as to overcome the principal technical obstruction to
applying the Hodge-Arakelov theory of [HASurI], [HASurII] [cf. Remark
4.3.1, (i)].
Note that the â€œdesired geometryâ€ in question will also be subject to other requirements. For instance, in [IUTchIII] [cf. also [IUTchII], Â§4], we shall make essential
use of the global arithmetic â€” i.e., the ring structure and absolute Galois groups â€”
of number ï¬elds. As observed above in Remark 4.3.1, (ii), these global arithmetic
structures are not compatible with the â€œarithmetic local-analytic sectionsâ€ constituted by the prime-strips. In particular, this state of aï¬€airs imposes the further
requirement that the â€œgeometryâ€ in question be compatible with globalization, i.e.,
that it give rise to the global arithmetic of the number ï¬elds in question in a fashion
that is independent of the various local geometries that appear in the â€œarithmetic
local-analytic sectionsâ€ constituted by the prime-strips, but nevertheless admits localization operations to these various local geometries [cf. Fig. 4.3; the discussion
of [IUTchII], Remark 4.11.2, (iii); [AbsTopIII], Remark 3.7.6, (iii), (v)].

Fig. 4.3: Globalizability
Finally, in order for the â€œdesired geometryâ€ to be applicable to the theory developed
in the present series of papers, it is necessary for it to be based on â€œeÌtale-like
structuresâ€, so as to give rise to canonical splittings, as in the eÌtale-picture discussed
in Corollary 3.9, (i). Thus, in summary, the requirements that we wish to impose
on the â€œdesired geometryâ€ are the following:
(a) local independence of global structures,
(b) globalizability, in a fashion that is independent of local structures,
(c) the property of being based on eÌtale-like structures.
Note, in particular, that properties (a), (b) at ï¬rst glance almost appear to contradict one another. In particular, the simultaneous realization of (a), (b) is highly

104

SHINICHI MOCHIZUKI

nontrivial. For instance, in the case of a function ï¬eld of dimension one over a
base ï¬eld, the simultaneous realization of properties (a), (b) appears to require
that one restrict oneself essentially to working with structures that descend to the
base ï¬eld! It is thus a highly nontrivial consequence of the theory of [AbsTopIII]
that the mono-anabelian geometry of [AbsTopIII] does indeed satisfy all of these
requirements (a), (b), (c) [cf. the discussion of [AbsTopIII], Â§I1].

Remark 4.9.1.
(i) Ultimately, in the theory of the present series of papers [cf., especially,
[IUTchII], Â§2], we shall be interested in
evaluating the eÌtale theta function of [EtTh] â€” i.e., in the spirit of
the Hodge-Arakelov theory of [HASurI], [HASurII] â€” at the various
D-prime-strips of â€  DJ , in the fashion stipulated by the labels discussed
in Proposition 4.7, (i).
These values of the eÌtale theta function will be used to construct various arithmetic
line bundles. We shall be interested in computing the arithmetic degrees â€” in
the form of various â€œlog-volumesâ€ â€” of these arithmetic line bundles. In order to
compute these global log-volumes, it is necessary to be able to compare the logvolumes that arise at D-prime-strips with diï¬€erent labels. It is for this reason that
the non-labeled output data of the functors of Proposition 4.9, (i), (ii), (iii) [cf. also
Proposition 4.11, (i), (ii), below], are of crucial importance in the theory of the
present series of papers. That is to say,
the non-labeled output data of the functors of Proposition 4.9, (i), (ii),
(iii) [cf. also Proposition 4.11, (i), (ii), below] â€” which allow one to
consider isomorphisms between the D-prime-strips that were originally

INTER-UNIVERSAL TEICHMUÌˆLLER THEORY I

117

assigned diï¬€erent labels â€” make possible the comparison of objects
[e.g., log-volumes] constructed relative to diï¬€erent labels.
In Proposition 4.11, (i), (ii), below, we shall see that by considering â€œprocessionsâ€,
one may perform such comparisons in a fashion that minimizes the label indeterminacy that arises.
(ii) Since the F
l -symmetry that appears in Proposition 4.9, (i), is transitive,
it follows that one may use this action to perform comparisons as discussed in (i).
This prompts the question:
What is the diï¬€erence between this F
l -symmetry and the Sl -symmetry
of the output data of the functors of Proposition 4.9, (ii), (iii)?
In a word, restricting to the F
l -symmetry of Proposition 4.9, (i), amounts to the
imposition of a â€œcyclic structureâ€ on the index set J [i.e., a structure of F
l -torsor

on J]. Thus, relative to the issue of comparability raised in (i), this Fl -symmetry
allows comparison between â€” i.e., involves isomorphisms between the non-labeled
D-prime-strips corresponding to â€” distinct members of this index set J, without
disturbing the cyclic structure on J. This cyclic structure may be thought of as
a sort of combinatorial manifestation of the link to the global object â€  D that
appears in a D-NF-bridge. On the other hand,
in order to compare these D-prime-strips indexed by J â€œin the absoluteâ€ to D-prime-strips that have nothing to do with J, it is necessary to
â€œforget the cyclic structure on Jâ€.
This is precisely what is achieved by considering the functors of Proposition 4.9,
(ii), (iii), i.e., by working with the â€œfull Sl -symmetryâ€.

Remark 5.1.4. The various functorial reconstruction algorithms for number
ï¬elds discussed in Example 5.1 are based on the technique of Belyi cuspidalization, as applied in the theory of [AbsTopIII], Â§1. At a more concrete level, this
theory of [AbsTopIII], Â§1, may be thought of revolving around the point of view
that
elements of number ï¬elds may be expressed geometrically by means of
Belyi maps.
Moreover, if one thinks of such elements of number ï¬elds as â€œanalytic functionsâ€,
then the remainder of the theory of [AbsTopIII] [cf., especially, [AbsTopIII], Â§5]
may be thought of as a sort of theory of
â€œanalytic continuationâ€ of the â€œanalytic functionsâ€ constituted by elements of number ï¬elds in the context of the various logarithm maps
at the various localizations of these number ï¬elds.
This point of view is very much in line with the points of view discussed in Remarks 4.3.2, 4.3.3. Moreover, the geometric representation of elements of number
ï¬elds via Belyi maps [i.e., whose existence may be regarded as a reï¬‚ection of
the hyperbolic nature of the projective line minus three points] is reminiscent of â€”
indeed, may perhaps be regarded as an arithmetic analogue of â€” the â€œcategories
of hyperbolic analytic continuationsâ€, i.e., of copies of the upper half-plane
regarded as equipped with their natural hyperbolic metrics, discussed in the
â€œMotivating Exampleâ€ given in the Introduction to [GeoAnbd]. Here, it is perhaps
of interest to note that the inequalities â€œâ‰¤ 1â€ satisï¬ed by the derivatives [i.e., with
respect to the respective PoincareÌ metrics] of the holomorphic maps that appear
in this â€œMotivating Exampleâ€ in [GeoAnbd] are reminiscent of the monotonically
decreasing nature of the various â€œdegreesâ€ â€” i.e., over Q of the ramiï¬cation locus of
the endomorphisms of the projective line over Q â€” that appear in the construction
of Belyi maps [where we recall that this monotonic decreasing of degrees is the key
observation that allows one to obtain Belyi maps which are unramiï¬ed over the
projective line minus three points].

in the present series of papers as the conjugate synchronization of local Galois
groups that will be discussed in [IUTchII], [IUTchIII], it has the following interesting
consequence:

Here, we pause, however, to make the following observation: At ï¬rst glance, it may
appear as though the analogue obtained by Uchida of the Neukirch-Uchida theorem
for maximal solvable quotients of absolute Galois groups of number ï¬elds [reviewed,
for instance, in [GlSol], Â§3] â€” or, perhaps, some future mono-anabelian version
of this result of Uchida â€” may be applied, in the context of the â€œÎº-sol-Kummer
theoryâ€ just discussed, to reconstruct the ring structure on the number ï¬elds
involved [i.e., in the fashion of Example 5.1, (v)]. In fact, however,
such a â€œsolvable-Uchida-typeâ€ reconstruction is, in eï¬€ect, meaningless from the point of view of the theory of the present series of papers
since it is fundamentally incompatible with the localization operations that occur in the structure of a D-Î˜NF-Hodge theater â€” cf. the
discussion of Remarks 4.3.1, 4.3.2.
Indeed, such a compatibility with localization would imply that the reconstruction
of the ring structure may somehow be â€œrestrictedâ€ to the absolute Galois groups of
completions at nonarchimedean primes of a number ï¬eld, i.e., in contradiction to the
well-known fact that absolute Galois groups of such completions at nonarchimedean
primes admit automorphisms that do not arise from ï¬eld automorphisms [cf., e.g.,
[NSW], the Closing Remark preceding Theorem 12.2.7]. Finally, we note that this
incompatibility of â€œsolvable-Uchida-typeâ€ reconstructions of ring structures with the
theory of the present series of papers is also interesting in the context of the point
of view discussed in Remark 5.1.4: Suppose, for instance, that it was the case that
the outer action of the absolute Galois group of a number ï¬eld on the geometric fundamental group of a hyperbolic curve over the number ï¬eld in fact factors through
the maximal solvable quotient of the absolute Galois group. Then it is conceivable
that some sort of version of the mono-anabelian theory of [AbsTopIII], Â§1, for extensions of such a maximal solvable quotient by the geometric fundamental group
under consideration could be applied in the theory of the present series of papers to
give a reconstruction of the ring structure of a number ï¬eld that only requires the
use of such extensions and is, moreover, compatible with the localization operations
that occur in the various types of â€œHodge theatersâ€ that appear in the theory of
the present series of papers â€” a state of aï¬€airs that would be fundamentally at
odds with a quite essential portion of the â€œspiritâ€ of the theory of the present
series of papers, namely, the point of view of â€œdismantling the two underlying
combinatorial dimensions of a ringâ€. In fact, however,
the outer action referred to above does not admit such a â€œsolvable factorizationâ€.
Indeed, the nonexistence of such a â€œsolvable factorizationâ€ is a formal consequence
of the the Galois injectivity result discussed in [NodNon], Theorem C â€” a result
that depends, in an essential way, on the theory of Belyi maps. Put another way,

134

SHINICHI MOCHIZUKI

Belyi maps not only play the role of allowing one to perform the sort of
â€œarithmetic analytic continuation via Belyi cuspidalizationsâ€ [i.e.,
discussed in Remark 5.1.4] that is of central importance in the theory of
the present series of papers, but also play the role of assuring one that such
â€œarithmetic analytic continuationsâ€ cannot be extended to the case of
extensions associated to â€œsolvable factorizationsâ€ of outer actions of
the sort just discussed.

Finally, we consider assertion (iv). First, we recall that since automorphisms
of Dv = B temp (X v )0 necessarily arise from automorphisms of the scheme X v [cf.

end, let Î± âˆˆ Ker(Aut(F v ) â†’ Aut(Dv )). For simplicity, we suppose [without loss of
generality] that Î± lies over the identity self-equivalence of Dv . Then I claim that
to show that Î± is [isomorphic to â€” cf. Â§0] the identity self-equivalence of F v , it
suï¬ƒces to verify that
Î± induces [cf. [FrdI], Corollary 4.11; [EtTh], Proposition 5.1] the identity
on the rational function and divisor monoids of F v .

INTER-UNIVERSAL TEICHMUÌˆLLER THEORY I

145

Indeed, recall that since F v is a Frobenioid of model type [cf. [EtTh], Deï¬nition 3.6,
(ii)], it follows from [FrdI], Corollary 5.7, (i), (iv), that Î± preserves base-Frobenius
pairs. Thus, once one shows that Î± induces the identity on the rational function
and divisor monoids of F v , it follows, by arguing as in the construction of the
equivalence of categories given in the proof of [FrdI], Theorem 5.2, (iv), that the
various units obtained in [FrdI], Proposition 5.6, determine [cf. Remark 5.3.3 below;
the argument of the ï¬rst paragraph of the proof of [FrdI], Proposition 5.6] an
isomorphism between Î± and the identity self-equivalence of F v , as desired.
Thus, we proceed to show that Î± induces the identity on the rational function and divisor monoids of F v , as follows. In light of the category-theoreticity [cf.
[EtTh], Theorem 5.6] of the cyclotomic rigidity isomorphism of [EtTh], Proposition
5.5, the fact that Î± induces the identity on the rational function monoid follows
immediately from the naturality of the Kummer map [cf. the discussion of Remark
3.2.4; [FrdII], Deï¬nition 2.1, (ii)], which is injective by [EtTh], Proposition 3.2,
(iii) â€” cf. the argument of [EtTh], Theorem 5.7, applied to verify the categorytheoreticity of the Frobenioid-theoretic theta function. Next, we consider the eï¬€ect
of Î± on the divisor monoid of F v . To this end, let us ï¬rst recall that Î± preserves cuspidal and non-cuspidal elements of the monoids that appear in this divisor monoid
[cf. Remark 3.2.4, (vi); [EtTh], Proposition 5.3, (i)]. In particular, by considering
the non-cuspidal portion of the divisor of the Frobenioid-theoretic theta function
and its conjugates [each of which is preserved by Î±, since Î± has already been shown
to induce the identity on the rational function monoid of F v ], we conclude that Î±
induces the identity on the non-cuspidal elements of the monoids that appear in
the divisor monoid of F v [cf. [EtTh], Proposition 5.3, (v), (vi), for a discussion
of closely related facts]. In a similar vein, since any divisor of degree zero on an
elliptic curve that is supported on the torsion points of the elliptic curve admits a
positive multiple which is principal, it follows by considering the cuspidal portions
of divisors of appropriate rational functions [each of which is preserved by Î±, since
Î± has already been shown to induce the identity on the rational function monoid of
F v ] that Î± also induces the identity on the cuspidal elements of the monoids that
appear in the divisor monoid of F v . This completes the proof of assertion (iv). 
